<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer8629625616420141307">A heartstrong story of family and romance, tribulation and tenacity, set on the High Plains east of Denver.<br /><br />In the small town of Holt, Colorado, a high school teacher is confronted with raising his two boys alone after their mother retreats first to the bedroom, then altogether. A teenage girl—her father long since disappeared, her mother unwilling to have her in the house—</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8629625616420141307">A heartstrong story of family and romance, tribulation and tenacity, set on the High Plains east of Denver.<br /><br />In the small town of Holt, Colorado, a high school teacher is confronted with raising his two boys alone after their mother retreats first to the bedroom, then altogether. A teenage girl—her father long since disappeared, her mother unwilling to have her in the house—is pregnant, alone herself, with nowhere to go. And out in the country, two brothers, elderly bachelors, work the family homestead, the only world they've ever known.<br /><br />From these unsettled lives emerges a vision of life, and of the town and landscape that bind them together—their fates somehow overcoming the powerful circumstances of place and station, their confusion, curiosity, dignity and humor intact and resonant. As the milieu widens to embrace fully four generations, Kent Haruf displays an emotional and aesthetic authority to rival the past masters of a classic American tradition.<br /><br />Utterly true to the rhythms and patterns of life, <em>Plainsong</em> is a novel to care about, believe in, and learn from.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8629625616420141307">...more</a>

      </div>

<div class="communityQuestionAndAnswer">
<div class="communityQuestion">
<div class="questionText">
<a href="/questions/401224-what-s-the-story-with-not-using-quotation">What's the story with not using quotation marks?</a>
</div>
<div class="communityQAFooter questionFooter">
<ul class="questionFooterActions">
<li class="footerItem like">
<span id="like_container_community_question_401224" class="likeItContainer"><a href="/rating/voters/401224?resource_type=CommunityQuestion" rel="nofollow" id="like_count_community_question_401224"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a> · <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F77156.Plainsong" class="jsLike like_it" rel="nofollow">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class="footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp">
<a href="/questions/401224-what-s-the-story-with-not-using-quotation">9 months ago</a>
</li>
<li class="footerItem communityQuestionPermalink">
<a href="/questions/401224-what-s-the-story-with-not-using-quotation">See all 2 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class="allCommunityAnswers">
<div class="communityAnswer">
<div class="communityAnswerBody">
<div class="answererUserIcon">
<a href="/user/show/1843025-velma" class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto"><img src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/users/1287255581i/1843025._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" alt="" /></a>
</div>
<div class="communityAnswerTextContainer">
<span class="communityAnswererName">
<a href="/user/show/1843025-velma">Velma</a>
</span>
<span class="communityAnswerText">
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I believe that some authors prefer to write dialogue <em>sans</em> quotation marks to encourage (force) the reader to slow down the reading process in order to<a href="#" class="jsShow showAction">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I believe that some authors prefer to write dialogue <em>sans</em> quotation marks to encourage (force) the reader to slow down the reading process in order to increase understanding and savor the prose. An article discussing this idea can be found <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.salon.com/2009/12/23/quotation_marks/">here</a>. The author of the article doesn't care for the practice, but personally I think, in this case in particular, it supports Haruf's spare style.<br /><a href="#" class="jsHide hideAction">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_291179184">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/291179184" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5300620-steve-sckenda" class="left imgcol" title="Steve Sckenda"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1409027039p2/5300620.jpg" alt="Steve Sckenda" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/291179184?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 18, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5300620-steve-sckenda" class="user" title="Steve Sckenda">Steve Sckenda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Lovers of the Plains and Minimalism</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=music" class="actionLinkLite">music</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=rural" class="actionLinkLite">rural</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=series" class="actionLinkLite">series</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5300620-steve-sckenda?shelf=minimalism" class="actionLinkLite">minimalism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer291179184">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15934882255033731100"><a title="Kent Haruf" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16266.Kent_Haruf">Kent Haruf</a> tells us about two sets of brothers living on the High Plains of Eastern Colorado. Don’t think scenic Rocky Mountains; think flat Texas Panhandle. The minimalist writing of <a title="Plainsong (Plainsong, #1) by Kent Haruf" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77156.Plainsong__Plainsong___1_">Plainsong</a> mimics the unadorned music of the title as well as the sparse landscape that is home for its characters. <br /><br />Indeed, the interlocking storylines are melodious, not harmonious. Young brothers, Ike and Bobby, miss their mother, who has moved to Denver because she suffers from severe depression. As the boys cope</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15934882255033731100"><a title="Kent Haruf" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16266.Kent_Haruf">Kent Haruf</a> tells us about two sets of brothers living on the High Plains of Eastern Colorado. Don’t think scenic Rocky Mountains; think flat Texas Panhandle. The minimalist writing of <a title="Plainsong (Plainsong, #1) by Kent Haruf" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77156.Plainsong__Plainsong___1_">Plainsong</a> mimics the unadorned music of the title as well as the sparse landscape that is home for its characters. <br /><br />Indeed, the interlocking storylines are melodious, not harmonious. Young brothers, Ike and Bobby, miss their mother, who has moved to Denver because she suffers from severe depression. As the boys cope with the <em>absence</em> of a female, while being cared for by their schoolteacher father, Guthrie, they learn about death in the animal world and in the physical world. <br /><br />Bachelor ranchers, Raymond and Harold McPheron, understand cows, but the brothers know nothing about human females until a pregnant teenager moves in with them. I appreciated how these kindly, old men, so set in their routine, cope with the <em>presence</em> of a female, and I laughed with them as they tried to interest Victoria in farm reports and pork bellies. Their unconditional love for her and their willingness to change, moved me.<br /><br />In addition to the obvious parallels between the sets of brothers, parallels also exist between the animal world and the human world. In alternating chapters, cows get pregnant, humans get pregnant; cows go crazy, humans go crazy; a horse dies, a human dies.<br /><br />Light is a major presence in the novel. Haruf describes the light in almost every scene: sunlight; starlight; lamplight; cigarette light. I picture monks chanting plainsong as part of liturgical worship where the time of day is detectable only by the slant of the light through plain windows, and I suspect, that upon close reading, the structure of this book may mimic the Daily Hours. <br /><br />Haruf presents his story in a limited third person point of view, so the reader never has access to the internal thoughts of the characters. We know only their actions and spoken words. Personally, I like to get inside characters’ internal worlds, so this is not my preferred narrative structure.<br /><br />Haruf is a very good writer, but his style does not cater to my personal taste. I respect that Haruf adopts the minimalist tone which mimics the landscape of the High Plains where the land is dry and flat, and the sky and the sun are important complements to the barrenness. I am familiar with this unremarkable landscape but bear it no special affection-- thus, my mixed reaction to this book. Some prefer their land and their stories to be unadorned, whereas I prefer my land green and growing and my words lush and lyrical; moreover, I prefer polyphony to monophony. <br /><br />This is a matter of taste, so I heartily recommend this book to those who prefer minimalist writing, and I have tremendous respect for the skill of Haruf in writing like this. Haruf continues the story of these characters in <a title="Eventide (Plainsong, #2) by Kent Haruf" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/89884.Eventide__Plainsong___2_">Eventide</a>, and I admire his skill enough to read it, but I may wait until I am suffering a cloying sensation from too much baroque and stand in need of stark simplicity. To everything there is a season; some day, I will be in the mood for another Gregorian chant. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15934882255033731100">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1445811708">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1445811708" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1199525-cecily" class="left imgcol" title="Cecily"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1343028343p2/1199525.jpg" alt="Cecily" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1445811708?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 10, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1199525-cecily" class="user" title="Cecily">Cecily</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Cecily by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/5300620-steve-sckenda">Steve Sckenda</a>
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=american-canadian" class="actionLinkLite">american-canadian</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=landscape-protagonist" class="actionLinkLite">landscape-protagonist</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=read-only-cos-of-gr-friends" class="actionLinkLite">read-only-cos-of-gr-friends</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1445811708">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16260138785433733307"><img width="120" height="100" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1449258051i/17257466._SX540_.jpg" /><br />Colorado High Plains, image source: <a rel="nofollow" href="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Colorado_High_Plains_Dirt_Road.JPG/1200px-Colorado_High_Plains_Dirt_Road.JPG" target="_blank">https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedi...</a><br /><br /><br /><strong>Absence</strong><br /><br />Peek between the gaps to appreciate the pared-down beauty of this book. At its heart, is absence: the words not used, the thoughts not made explicit, the loved-ones left or lost, the open space in which it is set. <br /><br />The language is plain, utilitarian, and unsentimental, as are the situations described in the short chapters.<br /><br />There is no backstory, there are no inner monologues - indeed, most of the characters say litt</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16260138785433733307"><img width="120" height="100" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1449258051i/17257466._SX540_.jpg" /><br />Colorado High Plains, image source: <a rel="nofollow" href="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Colorado_High_Plains_Dirt_Road.JPG/1200px-Colorado_High_Plains_Dirt_Road.JPG" target="_blank">https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedi...</a><br /><br /><br /><strong>Absence</strong><br /><br />Peek between the gaps to appreciate the pared-down beauty of this book. At its heart, is absence: the words not used, the thoughts not made explicit, the loved-ones left or lost, the open space in which it is set. <br /><br />The language is plain, utilitarian, and unsentimental, as are the situations described in the short chapters.<br /><br />There is no backstory, there are no inner monologues - indeed, most of the characters say little enough out loud (and when they do, there are no quotation marks) – and there are few suggestions as to what the future might hold. It is all rooted in the fickle but ubiquitous Colorado dust of the here and now (published in 1999, and maybe set a few years earlier).<br /><br />Simplicity lends clarity and focus, but what lies beneath is more complex.<br /><br />The story juxtaposes intolerable loss and cruelty with the amazing, unconditional generosity and love of virtual strangers. <br /><br />Despite the pain, there is always tender, tentative hope: "They knew they were not out of the woods yet, but they <strong>allowed</strong> themselves to <strong>believe</strong> that what they saw ahead was <strong>at least</strong> a <strong>faint</strong> track <strong>leading</strong> to a <strong>kind of promising</strong> clearing." So many caveats in a single sentence.<br /><br /><strong>Pairs</strong><br /><br />There is an unsettling sense of oscillation, uncertainty, precarious balance, because this is a book of pairs: twins and opposites, of people and situations – even pairs of pairs (Ike and Bobby Guthrie, the young brothers; Raymond and Harold McPheron, the old ones). <br /><br />One pair are bemused by the many varieties of sugar, eggs, and oats in a shop, then in the next chapter, another pair are bemused by the permutations of cribs, mattresses, and bedding. <br /><br />Windows are open and shaded. Two women withdraw from motherhood as another embarks on it, and childless people parent a mother. The pregnancy of cows is balanced with that of a young woman. Cruelty is counteracted with kindness. There is sleep and wakefulness, death and birth, light and dark, blood and beauty, and walking/pushing away versus being welcomed unconditionally. <br /><br />Perhaps the ultimate pair is that of this book with its sequel, Eventide. They are two halves of the same story, set a couple of years apart, both woven around the delicate combination of pairs of similarity and difference. If you read Plainsong and enjoy it, I urge to read Eventide soon after, as I did. Benediction looks more separate, despite GR labelling it as #3. See my reviews:<br /><br />•	<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1453096387">Eventide</a> 5*<br />•	<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1453096421">Benediction</a> TBR<br /><br /><br /><strong>Sleep</strong><br /><br />The hypnotic beauty of the plain song is almost soporific. Almost. <br /><br />Sleep is mentioned on the very first page, and is significant in almost every chapter for at least the first third of the book, and occasionally, but importantly, in the remainder. A crucial turning point – more than would ordinarily be the case – concerns the decision to buy a crib.<br /><br />There are so many dilemmas and issues: where characters sleep; how they sleep; whether to wake someone; whether someone is really asleep or not; the dishevelled look on waking; using sleep as an excuse; using <strong>lack</strong> of sleep as an excuse; watching someone sleep. However, who people sleep with (and don’t) is much less important.<br /><br />A tired character looked “like some survivor of a train wreck or flood, the sad remnant from some disaster that had passed through and done its damage and gone on”.<br /><br />It’s not just people: even the battered windows and screens of an abandoned house make it look “sleepy-eyed”. <br /><br />Individually these incidents are dismissible as quotidian, but taken together, they hint at something deeper: Holt is not a sleepy small town in the usual sense. <br /><br />There are life-changing events for all the main characters, but they are never treated as big emotional dramas. Holt's citizens are troubled in many ways, and that is reflected in their night-time hours. When they’re awake, they get on with life as best they can. <br /><br /><strong>Maggie Jones</strong><br /><br />Her name may head only one chapter, but she’s the benevolent puppeteer of most of the key characters. (The chapters each bear the title of the main character(s) concerned. All but two are: (Tom) Guthrie, Ike and Bobby (his sons), Victoria Roubideaux, and McPherons (elderly brothers). There is one each for Ella (Guthrie’s wife) and Maggie Jones.)<br /><br />She is the one who suggests the McPherons take in the pregnant teen, Victoria: “You solitary old bastards need somebody too… You’re going to die someday without ever having had enough trouble in your life. Not of the right kind anyway.” <br /><br />Then she steps back and leaves them to sort things out, not because she can’t be bothered or doesn’t care, but because she understands that’s the most effective way. <br /><br />Her involvement with Tom Guthrie is a similar combination of the forthright and passive.<br /><br /><strong>Stoner</strong><br /><br />The McPheron brothers reminded me strongly of the eponymous character of my (joint) favourite novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1108487558">Stoner</a>. If Stoner had followed his more likely destiny as a son of the soil, he would have been barely distinguishable from Harold and Raymond.<br /><br /><strong>Quotes</strong><br /><br />Important things happen to the characters in Plainsong, but it's really about the atmosphere conjured by the landscape and language, rather than plot.<br /><br />Haruf’s style is perfect for dirt and dust, slanted light and shadow.<br /><br />•	“The sun was higher, the light beginning to slide down the ladder of the windmill brightening it, making rings of rose-gold.”<br /><br />•	“The pickup lifted a powdery plume from the road and the suspended dust shone like bright flecks of gold in the sun.”<br /><br />•	“Her hair had been dyed, built not recently: her hair was maroon, like no human natural colour anywhere.”<br /><br />•	“The air was turning sharp, with a fall feeling of loneliness coming. Something unaccountable pending in the air.”<br /><br />•	“You must be lonely.”…… “They didn’t know how to say anything about that.”<br /><br />•	A pregnancy test packet with a picture of a “young honey-haired woman with the look of religious exaltation on her face and the sunshiny garden stretching out behind”.<br /><br />•	“The flicker of light spilled out into the side yard, where it flickered ever more faintly in a kind of illuminated echo on the dirt and dry weeds.”<br /><br />•	“Glancing out of the window toward the place where the sun shone aslant on a few bare trees risen up along the street. It looked cold and bleak outside.”<br /><br />•	“The trees along the curb… still showed sunlight at the tops; in the slanting afternoon sun the trees cast the thinnest of shadows as though they had been sprayed onto the street and brown grass.”<br /><br />•	“The empty house… The broken –down neglected locust trees, shaggy barked, the overgrown yard, the dead sunflowers grown up everywhere with their heads loaded and drooping, everything dry and brown now in the late fall, dust-coated, and the sunken house itself diminished and weathered.”<br /><br />•	“The little front room where at any time day or night, her clothes had been discarded and draped over the anonymous furniture and where… bowels of shrinking drying food had been put down at random on the bare rug.”<br /><br />•	“The sky had turned faint and wispy and the thin blue shadow had reached across the snow.”<br /><br />•	“Her eyes still bore a kind of wounded fierceness, as though the sadness and anger were both just below the surface.”<br /><br />•	“Only a thin violet band of light showed in the west on the low horizon.''<br /><br />•	“She acted as though she were happy… They watched her with their heads down and smiled when she said things.”<br /><br />•	“They looked as stiff and motionless as… two lifelike statues of minor saints.” (Note “minor”.)<br /><br />•	Wind “crying around the house corners, heaving and whinging in the bare trees.”<br /><br />•	“Too humbled and embarrassed to say what it was they wanted even if they could have said exactly what it was.”<br /><br />•	“If you can read you can cook. You can always feed yourselves. You remember that. I’m not just talking about here. When you go home too.”<br /><br />•	“The early dark of late December. The low sky closing down.”<br /><br />•	“All flat and sandy, the stunted stands of trees at the isolated farmhouses… The winter wheat was the only real green.”<br /><br />•	“The freckled shade of the trees… with the sun sliding down behind them.”<br /><br />(It’s possible I’ve instinctively added some of the commas Haruf deliberately omitted.)<br /><br />This is another book I loved, that I only knew about thanks to the review of a GR friend. Thank you, Steve.<br /><br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_3661656">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3661656" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/139822-dave-clapper" class="left imgcol" title="Dave Clapper"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1195886568p2/139822.jpg" alt="Dave Clapper" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/3661656?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 28, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/139822-dave-clapper" class="user" title="Dave Clapper">Dave Clapper</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Nance, Kath, Lena, anyone</span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer3661656">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7052225755444625498">Oh, what a beautiful book. Haruf's language is so deceptively simple--there's probably not a word in the book beyond sixth grade reading level. But with this simple language, he creates such beautiful, sad, lonely, human people. I'm particularly in love with the McPheron brothers, two elderly bachelor farmer brothers (and they're the single largest reason I think Nance needs to read this book).<br /><br />Something else about the simplicity of the language--I can't recall a single time that Haruf directly s</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7052225755444625498">Oh, what a beautiful book. Haruf's language is so deceptively simple--there's probably not a word in the book beyond sixth grade reading level. But with this simple language, he creates such beautiful, sad, lonely, human people. I'm particularly in love with the McPheron brothers, two elderly bachelor farmer brothers (and they're the single largest reason I think Nance needs to read this book).<br /><br />Something else about the simplicity of the language--I can't recall a single time that Haruf directly stated what a character was feeling. There were no internal dialogs, and no mentions of the words sad, happy, angry, or any of their synonyms (that I remember). Rather, Haruf simply relates their actions and their words between one another, and the reader makes a much stronger tie to what the characters are feeling. This may be the most literal rendering of the "show, don't tell" rule I've ever read.<br /><br />Many times, during the course of reading, I found myself pleading with the characters not to do what I knew they were going to do. But of course they did, not because a cheesy plot demanded it, but because it was the only thing these oh-so-very-real characters would do in the circumstances.<br /><br />The final chapter was a bit of a let-down, I have to admit--it tied things up a bit neatly for my tastes, especially considering how very, very real every moment to that point felt. But it wasn't enough of a let-down to make this anything less than a five star book. Highly, highly recommended. Beautiful, sad, human, and hopeful.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7052225755444625498">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1068271382">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1068271382" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4146473-connie" class="left imgcol" title="Connie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1449927088p2/4146473.jpg" alt="Connie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1068271382?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 08, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4146473-connie" class="user" title="Connie">Connie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4146473-connie?shelf=book-club-reads" class="actionLinkLite">book-club-reads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4146473-connie?shelf=favorites-i-recommend" class="actionLinkLite">favorites-i-recommend</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4146473-connie?shelf=pen-pal-reads" class="actionLinkLite">pen-pal-reads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4146473-connie?shelf=best-of-2014-5-stars" class="actionLinkLite">best-of-2014-5-stars</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4146473-connie?shelf=2014" class="actionLinkLite">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1068271382">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9326719363246811819"> Haruf's writing is as comforting to me as tomato soup and grilled cheese sandwiches on a cold winter day. For a reader who has memories of an idilic growing up when I read his stories I feel like I am going home and I am so glad there are stories that can take me there. The sounds, smells, voices and memories all come alive.<br /><br /> His prose is simple, yet so beautiful and paints such a vivid picture. His characters are earthy, there is no pretense about them, they are everyday people living ordinary</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9326719363246811819"> Haruf's writing is as comforting to me as tomato soup and grilled cheese sandwiches on a cold winter day. For a reader who has memories of an idilic growing up when I read his stories I feel like I am going home and I am so glad there are stories that can take me there. The sounds, smells, voices and memories all come alive.<br /><br /> His prose is simple, yet so beautiful and paints such a vivid picture. His characters are earthy, there is no pretense about them, they are everyday people living ordinary lives. This story describes small town Americana down to every detail. I fall for his characters and within a few pages I am invested in the story that is unfolding in through his words. They could be my neighbors, my family....they could be me.<br /><br /> In Plainsong, there were many characters that were easy to love.....the young people particularly. They still had a kind of awe at the world around them. The "adults" had some tarnish on them, were a bit rough about the edges due life lived, but their hearts and kindness came through. These characters have their own problems, yet as their lives intertwine in unexpected ways they form almost a family bond. There isa quietness, a wisdom and a lesson in his writing.<br /><br /> Haruf does not write to everyone's taste. There is no action, no mystery, no mayhem nor high drama. There is a beautiful story that makes you look around and appreciate the small things that come into your life. He allows you to escape to a simpler time when life didn't seem so large and so unconquerable. <br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9326719363246811819">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14618319966774878351">This is the book that sneaks up on you...<br /><br />I didn't realize how invested I was in the characters and their stories, until the author dangles their safety and lives in front of me and I find myself reading in a frenzy to ensure that everything is ok. Sneaky, sneaky.<br /><br />Holt, Colorado is a small town; seemingly everybody knows everybody and everybody's business. Couple of mundane, typical stories slowly thread themselves into your heart and realize that you've come to love each and every one of them in</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14618319966774878351">This is the book that sneaks up on you...<br /><br />I didn't realize how invested I was in the characters and their stories, until the author dangles their safety and lives in front of me and I find myself reading in a frenzy to ensure that everything is ok. Sneaky, sneaky.<br /><br />Holt, Colorado is a small town; seemingly everybody knows everybody and everybody's business. Couple of mundane, typical stories slowly thread themselves into your heart and realize that you've come to love each and every one of them in a different way. From Victoria- the girl you want to stand up for herself, to Ike &amp; Bobby the brothers who have seen much more than they can understand, and still protray the innocence of living in a small town. Tom &amp; the Pheron Brothers also have a soft spot in my heart-- standing up for their beliefs and making things right.<br /><br />This book isn't going to wow your socks off, or make you jump on your bed for joy. It's more like that comfy go-to old blanket that you grab on a cold night to wrap around your shoulders. It felt like a story I had read before, yet couldn't quite put my finger on which one it was...<br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer971205634">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8965948251474526569">This is an endearing story that is spun from the fabric of small town life in Holt, Colorado. Regular, decent folk at their finest, with just a few bad eggs thrown in for seasoning and texture. If this had been sappy in any way, I would have run for the hills, screaming. Let me assure you, there is an utter dearth of sappiness here, just a lovely story that will make your heart grow two sizes bigger. Not sure how such spare, simple writing managed to penetrate my stony exterior, but it did. I re</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8965948251474526569">This is an endearing story that is spun from the fabric of small town life in Holt, Colorado. Regular, decent folk at their finest, with just a few bad eggs thrown in for seasoning and texture. If this had been sappy in any way, I would have run for the hills, screaming. Let me assure you, there is an utter dearth of sappiness here, just a lovely story that will make your heart grow two sizes bigger. Not sure how such spare, simple writing managed to penetrate my stony exterior, but it did. I really cared about these people and the parallels contained within the pages were striking. <br /><br />As an aside, once upon a time, copies were made with mimeograph machines. You know, the ones with the manual crank thing and purple ink? There was a brief mention of those old ditto machines and it brought back happy memories of lifting those pieces of paper handed out by the teacher and inhaling deeply. I can almost smell it to this very day. Almost everyone did it. Really. </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer572970332">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13900380423198725540">In <strong>Plainsong</strong> Kent Haruf tells a <em>dam good</em> yarn with true-to-life characters and their everyday struggles living in rural Colorado.<p>The two wacky and wonderful <strong>old</strong> brothers who readily welcome a 17 year old <em>(pregnant)</em> teen into their home captured my heart early on as well as the two <strong>young</strong> curious brothers, abandoned by their mother, who befriend a lonely <strong>old</strong> woman, experience some pretty hard knocks and learn some facts of life by peering thru the window of an abandoned old shed.</p><p>While <a title="Our Souls at Night by Kent Haruf" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23602562.Our_Souls_at_Night">Our Souls at</a></p></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13900380423198725540">In <strong>Plainsong</strong> Kent Haruf tells a <em>dam good</em> yarn with true-to-life characters and their everyday struggles living in rural Colorado.<p>The two wacky and wonderful <strong>old</strong> brothers who readily welcome a 17 year old <em>(pregnant)</em> teen into their home captured my heart early on as well as the two <strong>young</strong> curious brothers, abandoned by their mother, who befriend a lonely <strong>old</strong> woman, experience some pretty hard knocks and learn some facts of life by peering thru the window of an abandoned old shed.</p><p>While <a title="Our Souls at Night by Kent Haruf" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/23602562.Our_Souls_at_Night">Our Souls at Night</a> remains my favorite Haruf novel thus far, the simplicity of his writing style entices me to read more.</p></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1268112243">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1268112243" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1384944-ron-charles" class="left imgcol" title="Ron Charles"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1254061135p2/1384944.jpg" alt="Ron Charles" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1268112243?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 05, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1384944-ron-charles" class="user" title="Ron Charles">Ron Charles</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1412050.Plainsong" title="Hardcover 9780375406188" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1268112243">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13502687280830857661">The glamour of the National Book Awards ceremony blows away the fusty air of the book world every November. Big publishers buy up acres of the banquet hall at the Marriott Marquis in Times Square. Little houses splurge a year's publicity budget on the $1,000-a-plate dinner. Even without the medals hanging from their necks, the nominees would stick out: New authors appear in ill-advised outfits, like red Nehru jackets or brocade gowns inspired by "King Lear," choices probably pushed on these quie</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13502687280830857661">The glamour of the National Book Awards ceremony blows away the fusty air of the book world every November. Big publishers buy up acres of the banquet hall at the Marriott Marquis in Times Square. Little houses splurge a year's publicity budget on the $1,000-a-plate dinner. Even without the medals hanging from their necks, the nominees would stick out: New authors appear in ill-advised outfits, like red Nehru jackets or brocade gowns inspired by "King Lear," choices probably pushed on these quiet, pensive writers by family members who insist they live it up for once. Famous authors in their own tuxedos or black dresses with mile-long shawls look mildly bored amid a swirl of friends and flacks. Really famous nominees don't show up, ensuring the most dramatic presence of all.<br /><br />Kent Haruf couldn't have looked more uncomfortable amid all this glitz in 1999. A teacher at Southern Illinois University in Carbondale, he was there because his third novel, "Plainsong," had been nominated. Amid the New York literati, he had the demeanor of a man who was skeptical of the hoopla, a little embarrassed by all the attention, but too humble to do anything but endure until he could get back home.<br /><br />The author is a good match with his work. "Plainsong" didn't win that night (the honor went to "Waiting," by Ha Jin), but it spent months on the bestseller list, gathering fans who responded to this quiet story about a little town on the High Plains east of Denver.<br /><br />Now comes a sequel called "Eventide" with a quarter-million first printing and a well-timed made-for-TV version of "Plainsong" broadcast last month on CBS. Mr. Haruf should prepare to be uncomfortable again: This gathering storm of publicity is entirely deserved, no matter how incongruous it is with his stark and simple tale.<br /><br />Readers of "Plainsong" will enter "Eventide" running, but newcomers needn't worry about picking up here. Once again, the story rotates through the lives of several families in Holt, Colo., most of whom appeared earlier. At the center are the McPheron brothers, crusty ranchers trying to keep stiff upper lips as they help Victoria and her baby pack for college. Two years ago, they took in Victoria when her mother threw her out for getting pregnant, and though the four of them made a strange and awkward family, the old men can't imagine life without her now.<br /><br />In town, 17 miles away from the McPheron ranch, Luther and Betty Wallace struggle to negotiate the complexities of food stamps and utility bills, child rearing and medical care. The family's precarious equilibrium is easily jostled by trouble at school or a visit from Betty's violent uncle. Their social worker never flags, though she's desperate to numb herself to the trouble in Holt County. She's always ready to serve as their counselor, financier, or chauffeur if it will keep them together, but she can't suspend the sense of doom that hovers over these mentally impaired parents.<br /><br />In fact, "Eventide" presents a grim sampling of family life. Eleven-year-old DJ cares for his infirm grandfather all alone, supervising the old man's monthly trips to the tavern as best he can. Next door, Mary Wells tries to maintain a pleasant home for her three little girls, but with no job and no word from her husband, the economics of survival grind away that hope.<br /><br />This hardscrabble story kicks up a dust cloud of melancholy that will sting even the most hardened readers' eyes. The fractured families that Haruf portrays - particularly the wary children - live in a world without any of the financial and social supports familiar to people who can drop $24.95 for a novel. A touch of maudlin pity would have soiled the effect, but Haruf never gives us the easy comfort of feeling superior.<br /><br />The relentless assaults of illness, meanness, or bad luck blow some of these people into oblivion. Angry cattle can maim, so can drunken uncles. But there are countervailing forces in this sparse Colorado landscape. You can see evidence in the comforting silence of the McPheron brothers' chores, in the extra effort of the Wallaces' social worker, and in neighbors' readiness to step in when routines are shattered. There are currents of affection here more persistent than strong, but ultimately capable of etching even the hard rock of these people's lives.<br /><br />It works only because Haruf describes their ordinary tragedies in prose that's strikingly unadorned. Their struggles are raised by this clarity to such an extraordinary vision that at the end of some chapters I was left wondering, Who in America can still write like this? Who else has such confidence and humility?<br /><br />Quotations don't do it justice, anymore than a tuft of prairie grass could convey the grandeur of an open plain. Every decoration has been stripped away, leaving a narrative that almost never hazards an interior thought or authorial comment, forcing the story to rest entirely on Haruf's flawless selection of detail and ear for dialogue.<br /><br />This is easy to do badly, as a thousand Hemingway imitators know, but Haruf never missteps, and I wish his books were required reading for anyone learning to write. Not that everyone should sound like him, of course, but his prose serves as a corrective to the super-hip, self-consciously clever storytelling that lures too many writers onto the rocks. After all, not everyone can write like Zadie Smith. (Even Zadie Smith can't always write like Zadie Smith.)<br /><br />"Eventide" never swells with climactic tragedy or heartrending triumph. Haruf holds the pace of his narrative to the slow passage of winter on the plains, letting moments of salvation thaw between hard frosts. But when Raymond McPheron finally finds the comfort he's lived without for so long, it's an affirmation of his nobility and patience that's utterly believable, quietly reassuring.<br /><br />This review first appeared in The Christian Science Monitor: <br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.csmonitor.com/2004/0504/p15s01-bogn.html" target="_blank">http://www.csmonitor.com/2004/0504/p1...</a><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13502687280830857661">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3622067847212544503">This is a quietly beautiful book. I think most Americans -- and maybe others -- are suckers for well-told tales of small town life, even though small towns, and their virtues and vices, are quickly disappearing. This story has finely drawn characters and is centered on the life a high school teacher who is raising his two boys pretty much single handedly as his wife sinks into depression. But the hopeful thread of the story lives in the tale of a young woman who becomes pregnant and must seek he</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3622067847212544503">This is a quietly beautiful book. I think most Americans -- and maybe others -- are suckers for well-told tales of small town life, even though small towns, and their virtues and vices, are quickly disappearing. This story has finely drawn characters and is centered on the life a high school teacher who is raising his two boys pretty much single handedly as his wife sinks into depression. But the hopeful thread of the story lives in the tale of a young woman who becomes pregnant and must seek help to avoid spiraling into rejection and poverty. The way the community handles that issue is what gives Plainsong its quiet optimism</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/461439970?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 10, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2752994-dem" class="user" title="Dem">Dem</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer461439970">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15838107913016459710"><strong> 3.5 stars </strong><br />Plainsong by Kent Haruf is a beautifully written novel and a nice easy read. This novel tells a simple story describing the lives of some of the residents in Holt, Colorado. <br />Tom Guthrie is a history teacher at the area high school. He is left to raise two sons Ike and Bobby after his wife abandons him and their family. <br />Victoria, a 17 year-old high school senior, is thrown out of her house by her crude and bitter mother after she had learned that her daughter is pregnant. <br />The McPheron</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15838107913016459710"><strong> 3.5 stars </strong><br />Plainsong by Kent Haruf is a beautifully written novel and a nice easy read. This novel tells a simple story describing the lives of some of the residents in Holt, Colorado. <br />Tom Guthrie is a history teacher at the area high school. He is left to raise two sons Ike and Bobby after his wife abandons him and their family. <br />Victoria, a 17 year-old high school senior, is thrown out of her house by her crude and bitter mother after she had learned that her daughter is pregnant. <br />The McPheron Brothers are two bachelors brothers who live out on their farm and who have little communication with the community.<br /><br />For me Plainsong was an enjoyable an easy read and I loved the characters and the atmosphere of the novel. However I did have a couple of issues with the novel and the first one was trying to figure the time setting of the story, as starting out I figured it was set perhaps 50/60s and further along I would have set it in the 80s.<br />It did leave me a little frustrated. <br /><br />I also was not convinced by the relationship between Victoria and the McPheron Brothers. The fact that two elderly bachelors would take in a pregnant 17 year old girl was a little bit farfetched for me.<br /><br />This novel is certainly worth reading, there are no major plot twists and no real surprises however the writing is so beautiful and certainly worthy of the praise that this book has received.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15838107913016459710">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5956877929698112105">Victoria Robideaux is 17 and pregnant. Her mother throws her out and she is taken in, for a time, by the kindly Maggie Jones. But Maggie’s old father is off his rocker and this makes for a dangerous household for Vicky. She winds up with two brothers, Raymond and Harold McPheron, sheltered gentlemen who have spent their entire lives working the same ranch in the home in which they grew up. The love that springs up in this makeshift family is a glory to behold. Dark forces abide in the town as we</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5956877929698112105">Victoria Robideaux is 17 and pregnant. Her mother throws her out and she is taken in, for a time, by the kindly Maggie Jones. But Maggie’s old father is off his rocker and this makes for a dangerous household for Vicky. She winds up with two brothers, Raymond and Harold McPheron, sheltered gentlemen who have spent their entire lives working the same ranch in the home in which they grew up. The love that springs up in this makeshift family is a glory to behold. Dark forces abide in the town as well, and are given their due attention, as well. But it is a very human town that Haruf portrays. The writing is beautiful and spare. This is a work of art. </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer194676584">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10183029751100588761">A graceful, quiet yet powerful book. It is a study of small town life, told through several characters in alternating chapters. There is nothing new here -- just everyday lives with the usual problems. Some people have more good than bad in them, some have more bad than good, and a lot of people just don't know how to balance any of that. The crusty old bachelor brother farmers in particular were wonderful characters.<br />Haruf has an extraordinary ability to create characters that speak directly fro</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10183029751100588761">A graceful, quiet yet powerful book. It is a study of small town life, told through several characters in alternating chapters. There is nothing new here -- just everyday lives with the usual problems. Some people have more good than bad in them, some have more bad than good, and a lot of people just don't know how to balance any of that. The crusty old bachelor brother farmers in particular were wonderful characters.<br />Haruf has an extraordinary ability to create characters that speak directly from the pages. Not schmaltzy. Just lovely. Pure storywriting.</span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer31365203">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18143980982905946712">Plainsong, and its sequel Eventide, are both beautifully written stories of about simple honest people trying to live their lives as best they can. I cannot overemphasize how well-written these books are. The narrator never intrudes on the story, which may be the best sign of a good writer. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_38504048">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/38504048" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1013193-jill" class="left imgcol" title="Jill"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1391401243p2/1013193.jpg" alt="Jill" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/38504048?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 23, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1013193-jill" class="user" title="Jill">Jill</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer38504048">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer406813410613557250">I bought this book in Salem, but didn't read it until I was in Montana, which is fitting for the book. Read it again last week, and as it's been a couple of years, I got to enjoy again, as though for the first time, the evocative language of Haruf's writing.<br /><br />The texture of the language brought out the taste of a bitter winter Colorado wind, rushing along the flat, barren land. The plain spoken people were aptly described, rendering them not necessarily lovable, but realistic, living their lives i</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText406813410613557250">I bought this book in Salem, but didn't read it until I was in Montana, which is fitting for the book. Read it again last week, and as it's been a couple of years, I got to enjoy again, as though for the first time, the evocative language of Haruf's writing.<br /><br />The texture of the language brought out the taste of a bitter winter Colorado wind, rushing along the flat, barren land. The plain spoken people were aptly described, rendering them not necessarily lovable, but realistic, living their lives in the shadow of a rough landscape, where men are men, and so are the women. (Maybe that's Alaska, but you get my point.)<br /><br />Haruf weaves an altogether tragic, and yet still humorous story of the lives of several people in a small western town. The troubles that the boys run into throughout the course of the book are the worst part - tragedies that mature them faster than their 10 or 11 years. But the humor of the interaction of the older brothers is a wonderful counterpoint.<br /><br />The language is at times rough and graphic, but if it wasn't, it wouldn't be realistic. The ending could strike readers as sappy as well, but I thought it was fitting to describe a people who aren't downtrodden by all the distressing events in life, but rather who take them quietly, figuring it's their lot in life, no matter how fair or unfair.<br /><br />I loved it and highly recommend it.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="406813410613557250">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1312246527">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1312246527" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1628041-book-concierge" class="left imgcol" title="Book Concierge"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1324904606p2/1628041.jpg" alt="Book Concierge" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1312246527?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 19, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1628041-book-concierge" class="user" title="Book Concierge">Book Concierge</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1412050.Plainsong" title="Hardcover 9780375406188" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=concierge" class="actionLinkLite">concierge</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=library" class="actionLinkLite">library</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=book-club" class="actionLinkLite">book-club</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=relationships" class="actionLinkLite">relationships</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=literary-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=western" class="actionLinkLite">western</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1312246527">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5413719980460216587"><em>Plainsong – the unisonous vocal music used in the Christian church from the earliest times; any simple and unadorned melody or air</em><br /><br />This definition perfectly describes this gentle, unassuming yet impactful novel. Haruf introduces us to several residents of the small town of Holt, Colorado: Tom Guthrie, a high school teacher struggling to raise his two young sons (Ike and Bobby, ages 9 and 10) after their mother leaves; Victoria Robideaux, a pregnant teenager forced to try to make it on her own; th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5413719980460216587"><em>Plainsong – the unisonous vocal music used in the Christian church from the earliest times; any simple and unadorned melody or air</em><br /><br />This definition perfectly describes this gentle, unassuming yet impactful novel. Haruf introduces us to several residents of the small town of Holt, Colorado: Tom Guthrie, a high school teacher struggling to raise his two young sons (Ike and Bobby, ages 9 and 10) after their mother leaves; Victoria Robideaux, a pregnant teenager forced to try to make it on her own; the McPheron brothers – Harold and Raymond – elderly bachelors working the family cattle ranch; and Maggie Jones a compassionate woman who helps weave a crazy quilt of characters into a sort of family. <br /><br />With little in common but the realities of a hard life, these seven people hesitate to reach out to one another, but find comfort when they succeed. There are scenes of tenderness that took me by surprise – from the lonely old woman who recognizes the boys’ pain at losing their mother, to the way the bachelor brothers open their home and hearts to the youngest generation. And there are some violent scenes that had me nearly breathless with worry. <br /><br />The prose is crisp and clean as the winter air in the Great Plains. The story is at once simple and profound. Haruf gives us a glimpse at the lives of these wonderful characters – lives full of wit, sorrow, happy surprises, poor choices, impetuous actions, and thoughtful responses. At the end, I am left wanting more. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5413719980460216587">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_19320034">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19320034" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/739464-colin-mckay-miller" class="left imgcol" title="Colin McKay Miller"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442509875p2/739464.jpg" alt="Colin McKay Miller" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/19320034?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 15, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/739464-colin-mckay-miller" class="user" title="Colin McKay Miller">Colin McKay Miller</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/834286.Plainsong" title=" 9780330396639" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/739464-colin-mckay-miller?shelf=novels" class="actionLinkLite">novels</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/739464-colin-mckay-miller?shelf=book-club-picks" class="actionLinkLite">book-club-picks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer19320034">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer204059450173473790">EDIT: Updated the description to appease comment super fans.<br /><br />Plainsong is the story of intersecting lives in a small town in Colorado: A teacher (Tom Guthrie), his kids (Ike and Bobby), a pregnant 17-year-old (Victoria Roubideaux), and the elderly bachelor farmers (the McPherons). High school teacher Maggie Jones is the one character tying them together, but she is never given a (third-person) chapter viewpoint like the other main characters. Unlike most of Plainsong, this move is to Haruf’s cred</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText204059450173473790">EDIT: Updated the description to appease comment super fans.<br /><br />Plainsong is the story of intersecting lives in a small town in Colorado: A teacher (Tom Guthrie), his kids (Ike and Bobby), a pregnant 17-year-old (Victoria Roubideaux), and the elderly bachelor farmers (the McPherons). High school teacher Maggie Jones is the one character tying them together, but she is never given a (third-person) chapter viewpoint like the other main characters. Unlike most of Plainsong, this move is to Haruf’s credit. <br /><br />Plainsong is left for dead by cliché characters and plotlines. Every antagonist is so obviously flagged as vile that they no longer come off as real. The bad boy at school forces his girlfriend to have sex with other men, drives too fast, and insults everyone and everything, just like his parents. A gutsier (and quite frankly more talented) author would have given one of the antagonists a viewpoint, maybe even some reasoning, as to why they are the way that they are. Instead, Haruf sticks with bad guys wearing black. Don’t even get me started on the sleazy, harlequin romance novel lines. I think there’s an online generator for sexual euphemisms this bad. <br /><br />The saving grace lies mainly in the two sets of brothers and Maggie Jones. If it weren’t for her, the reader could get the feeling that women are only in this book so that someone could have (or already had) sex with them. Seriously. Maggie Jones is the lone female character with zest and likeability. The young brothers, Ike and Bobby, are written well, and even if they do seem to have too much responsibility for their age (blame the small town vibe), Haruf maintains the strength of their plotline without making them act older when need be. The older McPheron brothers are truly likeable, but could never be anything more than minor since they’re the strong, silent types. <br /><br />In general, I like stories with differing character viewpoints whose plotlines begin to overlap, but Kent Haruf’s Plainsong doesn’t have the character strength to pull off the small town story, and as a result of that, stands as a reminder that what works on a base level isn’t necessarily good. If it were an action piece, the lack of character development would be acceptable, but with the plot slow to develop and seemingly stretching past where the book ends, the story falls flat in too many areas. One star.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="204059450173473790">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_10955087">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10955087" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/307208-lucy" class="left imgcol" title="Lucy"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1233177432p2/307208.jpg" alt="Lucy" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/10955087?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 09, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/307208-lucy" class="user" title="Lucy">Lucy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer10955087">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12462892406906585410">The first page of this book has a definition of the word plainsong. It is:<br /><br />"any simple and unadorned melody or air."<br /><br />I appreciated this book more than I liked it. The author, Kent Haruf, writes with a vividly clear but simple prose about a small town in northeastern Colorado, a couple of hours from Denver, whose occupants struggle with their choices, their relationships and their opportunities.<br /><br />Kind of a universal story, honestly, but in this setting - so sparse and empty - Haruf managed to develop</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12462892406906585410">The first page of this book has a definition of the word plainsong. It is:<br /><br />"any simple and unadorned melody or air."<br /><br />I appreciated this book more than I liked it. The author, Kent Haruf, writes with a vividly clear but simple prose about a small town in northeastern Colorado, a couple of hours from Denver, whose occupants struggle with their choices, their relationships and their opportunities.<br /><br />Kind of a universal story, honestly, but in this setting - so sparse and empty - Haruf managed to develop characters that I felt like I knew. Perhaps it's growing up in Montana, where there are small towns to spare, but his book had a realness to it that felt uncomfortable to me. I had teachers like Tom Guthrie. Men who taught because it was a job, not because they were particularly suited for the profession. I was aware of girls like Victoria Roubideaux, young and troubled (in this case pregnant), misfits from the small town core.<br /><br />There are many characters throughout the book, some better developed than others. Tom Guthries' sons, Bobby and Ike, who want more than either of their parents are able to give them. The McPherson brothers, old bachelors who live alone on a farm. The teenage redhead, who frightens me and makes me angry until I realize he mirrors the attitudes and behaviors of his own parents. Maggie Jones, who is the poorest developed and thus the most likable. As the book approaches its halfway point, their lives start to intersect rather than orbit. Even as the intersections begin, lives aren't drastically changed or made better. There is some growth and a hint at tenderness, particularly when the old McPherson brothers take in Victoria, who is alone and pregnant at 17.<br /><br />When I finished the book, I snapped it shut and shouted, "Why do I read stuff like this?!" Days later, I still feel melancholy about Holt, Colorado and its inhabitants who seem to have so little going for them.<br /><br />As frustrated as I was by the end, because there was no resolution of conflict, no great triumph or lesson learned or bridges crossed, I can quietly nod "well done" to the author for staying true to the apparent purpose of his book. This is a story of "what is" more than "what happened."<br /><br />It is a plainsong.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12462892406906585410">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_566050526">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/566050526" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7481492-margitte" class="left imgcol" title="Margitte"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1361772481p2/7481492.jpg" alt="Margitte" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/566050526?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 06, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7481492-margitte" class="user" title="Margitte">Margitte</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7481492-margitte?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer566050526">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3635122622331402755">I really loved the serene quietness, the honor, integrity, kindness and caring in the book. An American story where the good people still matters. Where loneliness are the common factor in the character's lives and how it is addressed the good ole American way. We need this kind of books in the modern world. I don't know if Holt, Denver really exists, but I wouldn't mind living there amongst those special people! I'm giving it four stars for the atmosphere, message, and intention of the book.</span>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1459766548">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1459766548" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/35550351-piperitapitta" class="left imgcol" title="Piperitapitta"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1412245089p2/35550351.jpg" alt="Piperitapitta" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1459766548?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 16, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/35550351-piperitapitta" class="user" title="Piperitapitta">Piperitapitta</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/27855505-canto-della-pianura" title="Paperback 9788899253172" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/35550351-piperitapitta?shelf=romanzo" class="actionLinkLite">romanzo</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/35550351-piperitapitta?shelf=letteratura-usa" class="actionLinkLite">letteratura-usa</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/35550351-piperitapitta?shelf=autori-usa" class="actionLinkLite">autori-usa</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1459766548">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18427228409641573973"><strong>Io canto.</strong><br /><br />Storie la cui realtà è dura e sferzante come il vento freddo che soffia in inverno a Holt, Colorado.<br />Storie di gente semplice, di una comunità che ruota intorno a poche strade (la Main e la Second onnipresenti, la Chicago street, Railroad e poco altro, quasi che il paese, l'agglomerato, più che altro, sia tutto lì), al bestiame da marchiare, alla scuola dove insegna Tom Guthrie, alle giornate sempre uguali dei solitari fratelli McPheron - <em>scontrosi e ignoranti. Tristi. Indipendenti.</em> - e</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18427228409641573973"><strong>Io canto.</strong><br /><br />Storie la cui realtà è dura e sferzante come il vento freddo che soffia in inverno a Holt, Colorado.<br />Storie di gente semplice, di una comunità che ruota intorno a poche strade (la Main e la Second onnipresenti, la Chicago street, Railroad e poco altro, quasi che il paese, l'agglomerato, più che altro, sia tutto lì), al bestiame da marchiare, alla scuola dove insegna Tom Guthrie, alle giornate sempre uguali dei solitari fratelli McPheron - <em>scontrosi e ignoranti. Tristi. Indipendenti.</em> - e a quelle dei giovani e curiosi Ike e Bobby: anche loro fratelli, ma ancora poco più che bambini, che ogni giorno inforcano le loro bici per consegnare i giornali in quelle quattro case, affamati come sono di amore materno, selvatici come le vacche e i cavalli che ancora non hanno imparato ad addestrare.<br />E poi c'è Victoria, diciassette anni e incinta: senza padre, con una madre che l'ha buttata fuori di casa appena venuta a conoscenza del fattaccio. Una ragazza, e <em>le ragazze sono diverse. Vogliono cose. Hanno sempre bisogno di cose</em>. E Victoria ha bisogno di un tetto, soprattutto.<br />È la fame di amore, l'incapacità di dare e l'improvvisa capacità di abbracciare, invece, e di offrire protezione e riparo, a colpire in questo romanzo: la fame di amore, l'assenza di amore, l'incapacità di amare, il dono di amare...Ed è tutto un flusso vitale, di amore e di non amore, che circola e che permea le storie di Victoria, di Ike e Bobby, di Guthrie e dei fratelli McPheron, le cui vicende si alternano nei capitoli - caratteristica che sembra essere ormai costante della letteratura statunitense contemporanea - fino a costituire, attraverso le singole vicende, una storia unitaria.<br />Credevo di poter dire che è minimalista, la scrittura di Kent Haruf, finché non ho iniziato a leggere "Benedizione", terzo romanzo della "Trilogia della pianura", la "loose trilogy", come definisce la stesso autore (che La NNE pubblica in ordine sfuso, iniziando proprio dall'ultimo - ma racconta in maniera più che convincente, per me, qual è il motivo, in questa bella lettera aperta ai lettori <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.nneditore.it/lettera-aperta-ai-lettori-di-haruf/" target="_blank">http://www.nneditore.it/lettera-apert...</a>), per cui mi limiterò a parlare di sobrietà ed essenzialità, seguendo, e usando, le belle parole del traduttore Fabio Cremonesi (le sue note alla traduzione, per quanto brevi, sono un vero spasso, tanto da apparire quasi ingenuo nel suo confessare ai lettori entusiasmo, dubbi e aspettative) e di esattezza, un termine che rimanda al rigore francescano, scrive Cremonesi, ma anche alla luminosità dei paesaggi descritti da Haruf, da quello sguardo nitido e a volte anche brutale su cose e persone, che si tramuta in parole, e da parole quasi in canto: <em>Playsong</em>, il titolo originale, il <em>canto della pianura</em>, o anche il <em>canto piano</em> in una diversa accezione della traduzione che fa riferimento al <em>canto a cappella</em> (come quello del periodo Gregoriano), che nella mia personalissima interpretazione diventa anche un canto sommesso, che da Denver sale su verso gli altopiani, fino ad arrivare a Holt, in Colorado.<br /><br /><em>«Uscirono da Denver, allontanandosi dalle montagne per tornare sugli altopiani: artemisia e yucca e gramigna ed erba del bisonte nei pascoli, grano e mais nei campi. Ai due lati della Statale c'erano piste sterrate che correvano sotto il cielo terso, dritte come le righe di un libro, con poche cittadine isolatet sparse nella pianura sconfinata.»</em> (da «Benedizione»)</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18427228409641573973">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
